        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.7b12503"><meta property="og:title" content="Interconnected CoFe2O4–Polypyrrole Nanotubes as Anode Materials for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/acsami.7b12503.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="CoFe2O4-coated polypyrrole (PPy) nanotubes (CFO-PPy-NTs) with three-dimensional (3-D) interconnected networks have been prepared through a simple hydrothermal method. The application has been also studied for sodium ion batteries (SIBs). The finely crystallized CoFe2O4 nanoparticles (around 5 nm in size) are uniformly grown on the PPy nanotubes. When tested as anode materials for SIBs, the CFO-PPy-NT electrode maintains a discharge capacity of 400 mA h g–1 and a stable Coulombic efficiency of 98% after 200 cycles at 100 mA g–1. Even at a higher current density of 1000 mA g–1, the composite can still retain a discharge capacity of 220 mA h g–1 after 2000 cycles. The superior electrochemical performance could be mainly ascribed to the uniform distribution of CoFe2O4 on the 3-D matrix of PPy interconnected nanotubes, which favors the diffusion of sodium ions and electronic transportation and also buffers the large volumetric expansion during charge/discharge. Thereby our study suggests that such CFO-PPy-NTs have great potential as an anode material for SIBs."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/acsami.7b12503.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6e9957db7775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e9957db7775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'a085ed41-25a9-4063-ba8b-c275c5ee4a56'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90f6e9957db7775c-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e9957db7775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b12503&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acsami.7b12503" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite <script nonce="90f6e9957db7775c-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Interconnected CoFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;–Polypyrrole Nanotubes as Anode Materials for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b12503", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Interconnected CoFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;–Polypyrrole Nanotubes as Anode Materials for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b12503", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Interconnected CoFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;–Polypyrrole Nanotubes as Anode Materials for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b12503", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Interconnected CoFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;–Polypyrrole Nanotubes as Anode Materials for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b12503", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Interconnected CoFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;–Polypyrrole Nanotubes as Anode Materials for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b12503", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Interconnected CoFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;–Polypyrrole Nanotubes as Anode Materials for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b12503", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Interconnected CoFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;–Polypyrrole Nanotubes as Anode Materials for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b12503", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Interconnected CoFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;–Polypyrrole Nanotubes as Anode Materials for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b12503", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Interconnected CoFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;–Polypyrrole Nanotubes as Anode Materials for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b12503", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

R. China</span></div><div class="corresp-info"><strong>*</strong>Tel: +86-21-52411704; Fax: +86-21-52413903; E-mail: <a href="mailto:zywen@mail.sic.ac.cn">zywen@mail.sic.ac.cn</a></div></div></div><div class="article_header-footer"><div class="article_header-footer-left"><div class="article_header-links"><input class="pub-doi" type="hidden" name="pub-doi" value="10.1021/acsami.7b12503"><a href="/doi/epdf/10.1021/acsami.7b12503?ref=article_openPDF" title="PDF" target="_blank" class="article__btn__secondary" data-id="article_header_OpenPDF"><i class="icon-open-pdf"></i><span>Open PDF</span></a><a href="#_i14" title="Supporting Information" class="article__btn__secondary internalNav" data-id="article_header_SupportingInfo"><i class="icon-supporting-info"></i><span>Supporting Information (1)</span></a><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=s0&amp;dbid=16384&amp;doi=10.1021/acsami.7b12503&amp;type=refOpenUrl&amp;url=http%3A%2F%2Fimp-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com%2Fopenurl%2F44IMP%2FICL_VU1%3Fid%3Ddoi%3A10.1021%252Facsami.7b12503%26sid%3Dliteratum%253Aachs%26pmid%3D28960062%26genre%3Darticle%26aulast%3DHe%26date%3D2017%26atitle%3DInterconnected%2BCoFe2O4%25E2%2580%2593Polypyrrole%2BNanotubes%2Bas%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BHigh%2BPerformance%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26jtitle%3DACS%2BApplied%2BMaterials%2B%2526%2BInterfaces%26title%3DACS%2BApplied%2BMaterials%2B%2526%2BInterfaces%26volume%3D9%26issue%3D42%26spage%3D36927%26epage%3D36935%26issn%3D1944-8244" title="OpenURL IMPERIAL COLLEGE LONDON" class="issue-item_open-url sfxLink"><img src="/userimages/9157ccea-6c85-4e5f-ac2a-6a9fd9422c17/sfxbutton-180e067c810" alt="OpenURL IMPERIAL COLLEGE LONDON"></a></div></div></div><div class="article_header-meta"><div class="article_header-meta-left"><input type="hidden" name="journalCodeForjhpLink" value="aamick"><input type="hidden" name="journalNameForjhpLink" value="ACS Applied Materials &amp; 2017</time></li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div class="hidden" property="articleSection">research-article</div><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D36927%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DQiming%2BHe%252C%2BKun%2BRui%252C%2BChunhua%2BChen%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D9%26issueNum%3D42%26contentID%3Dacsami.7b12503%26title%3DInterconnected%2BCoFe2O4%25E2%2580%2593Polypyrrole%2BNanotubes%2Bas%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BHigh%2BPerformance%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D36935%26publicationDate%3DOctober%2B2017" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/medium/am-2017-12503b_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/large/am-2017-12503b_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b12503&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>-coated polypyrrole (PPy) nanotubes (CFO-PPy-NTs) with three-dimensional (3-D) interconnected networks have been prepared through a simple hydrothermal method. The application has been also studied for sodium ion batteries (SIBs). The finely crystallized CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles (around 5 nm in size) are uniformly grown on the PPy nanotubes. When tested as anode materials for SIBs, the CFO-PPy-NT electrode maintains a discharge capacity of 400 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and a stable Coulombic efficiency of 98% after 200 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Even at a higher current density of 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the composite can still retain a discharge capacity of 220 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 2000 cycles. The superior electrochemical performance could be mainly ascribed to the uniform distribution of CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> on the 3-D matrix of PPy interconnected nanotubes, which favors the diffusion of sodium ions and electronic transportation and also buffers the large volumetric expansion during charge/discharge. Thereby our study suggests that such CFO-PPy-NTs have great potential as an anode material for SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D36927%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DQiming%2BHe%252C%2BKun%2BRui%252C%2BChunhua%2BChen%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D9%26issueNum%3D42%26contentID%3Dacsami.7b12503%26title%3DInterconnected%2BCoFe2O4%25E2%2580%2593Polypyrrole%2BNanotubes%2Bas%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BHigh%2BPerformance%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D36935%26publicationDate%3DOctober%2B2017" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Composites" class="article__tags__link">Composites</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290920&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanoparticles" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanoparticles" class="article__tags__link">Nanoparticles</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290637&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanotubes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanotubes" class="article__tags__link">Nanotubes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291503&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Organic polymers" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Organic%20polymers" class="article__tags__link">Organic polymers</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cofe2o4&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_CoFe2O4">CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Polypyrrole&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_polypyrrole">polypyrrole</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nanotubes&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nanotubes">nanotubes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">1 Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Nowadays, large-scale stationary applications of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are severely hindered by limited reserves and uneven geographical distribution of lithium resources on the earth, both of which have caused a notable rise in the cost of LIBs. Based on the above concern, it is really necessary to develop new kinds of energy storage devices. Lately, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted enormous interest as promising alternatives for LIBs owing to the virtually inexhaustible sodium resources that are ubiquitous around the world along with their prominent advantage of cost efficiency. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1-4)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">One of the crucial challenges to realize the future commercialization of SIBs is to develop high performance anode materials of low-cost. Unfortunately, although some research work has made certain improvement, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 5 to 7">(5-7)</a> the discovery of viable anode materials still remains a big challenge, which hampers the overall performance of SIBs. A main reason is that Na<sup>+</sup> has a larger ionic radius (1.02 Å) and molar mass (22.99 g mol<sup>–1</sup>) compared to those of Li<sup>+</sup> (0.59 Å, 6.94 g mol<sup>–1</sup>), which will lead to larger volume changes during charge and discharge, making it hard to find suitable anode materials capable of rapid and stable sodium storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8, 9)</a> Recently iron-based oxide materials have been investigated as anode materials for SIBs due to their low cost, high sodium storage capacity, and good conductivity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 10 to 14">(10-14)</a> Spinel ferrites, with a composition of AFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (A = Zn, Ni, Co, Mn), are a series of iron-based oxide materials displaying greater performance than simple iron oxides. There are a few reports showing that binary oxides can deliver high capacity originating from the synergetic effect. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 15 to 17">(15-17)</a> The binary metals in this structure can form a buffer matrix to reduce the impact of volume change and retain structural stability during charge and discharge processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18, 19)</a> This matrix also maintains a relatively low activation energy for electron transfer, resulting in higher electrical conductivity and electrochemical activities than single oxides. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20, 21)</a> Based on the discussion above, the spinel ferrites hold great potentials to be high performance anode materials for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">In recent years, lots of efforts have been made to build three-dimensional (3-D) microstructure composites to solve the problem of volume expansion and shrinkage during cycling, such as anchoring nanoparticles onto graphene frameworks <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> or confining them in mesoporous carbon, thus to further enhancing the cycle stability and rate performance of anode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> As a type of conducting polymer, polypyrrole (PPy), which maintains a 3-D structure of interconnected nanotubes, can both act as a conducting agent and a supporting matrix to enhance the conductivity and improve the rate performance of the cell. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24, 25)</a> Apart from good structural stability and fast electron transport paths, this hierarchical interconnected microstructure has an enlarged active surface area to ensure good contact with the electrolyte, thus providing efficient open channels for rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transportation.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Here in this work, we prepared interconnected CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>–PPy nanotubes with a 3-D microstructure using an in situ hydrothermal synthesis method. In this composite, the CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles are uniformly anchored on the surface of PPy nanotubes. The as-prepared CFO-PPy-NTs showed good cycle performance. After 200 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, a reversible capacity of 400 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> was achieved, maintaining a Coulombic efficiency of 98%. Even when the current density was up to 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity still remained 220 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 2000 cycles. The performance in terms of sodium storage capacity for CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is extraordinary, and it exhibits high potential for future application in next-generation SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">2 Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3">2.1 Material Synthesis</h3><div id="sec2_1_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.1 Preparation of Polypyrrole Nanotubes (PPy-NTs)</h4><div class="NLM_p last">The PPy nanotubes were prepared according to a previous report <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> with minor modifications. First, 0.1 g of methyl orange (MO) was dissolved into 150 mL of deionized water to form a solution. Next, 1.96 g of FeCl<sub>3</sub> was added to the above solution under vigorous stirring, and a blood-red flocculent precipitate showed up simultaneously. After that, 0.484 g of pyrrole monomer was added into the mixture followed by continuous stirring for 24 h at ambient temperature. Finally, the as-synthesized PPy precipitate was washed with deionized water and ethanol several times and then dried under vacuum at 60 °C for 24 h.</div></div><div id="sec2_1_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.1.2 Preparation of CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>-Polypyrrole Nanotubes</h4><div class="NLM_p">To prepare the CFO-PPy-NTs, 0.22 g of hexamethylenetetramine (HMTA) and 0.18 g of urea were first dissolved into 76 mL of ethanol, and then 0.15 g of PPy was uniformly dispersed into the above solution by ultrasonication for 1 h. Subsequently, 0.40 g of Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O and 0.59 g of Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O were added. After Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Co<sup>2+</sup> salts were dissolved, the final blend was transferred into a 95 mL Teflon-lined autoclave and then heated and maintained at 120 °C for 10 h. The final CFO-PPy-NTs were obtained by washing the precipitates with deionized water and ethanol several times and dried under vacuum at 60 °C overnight.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles (CFO-NPs) were also prepared using the same procedure without adding PPy.</div></div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.2 Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) patterns were obtained by using a Rigaku Ultima IV diffractometer, with Cu Kα radiation at a scan rate of 6° min<sup>–1</sup> (2θ from 10 to 80°) to identify the phase and crystal structure of the as-synthesized samples. The weight ratio of PPy in the composite was characterized by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) using a NETZSCH STA 449C analyzer between 50 and 800 °C at a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> in air. The Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy characterization was conducted on a Bruker Tensor 27 FTIR analyzer. The valence state of the elements in the PPy and the composite was studied by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) using an ESCAlab250 system with a monochromatic Al Kα X-ray source. Field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM, SU8200, and Magellan 400) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM, JEM-2100F) were performed to characterize the morphology of the composite. A Tristar II 3020 specific surface area analyzer was used to measure the BET surface areas of PPy nanotubes and the composite.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.3 Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical performance testing was conducted using 2025-type coin cells. Na foil (Sigma-Aldrich) was exploited as a counter and reference electrode. Glass fiber was used as the separator. The working electrode was fabricated by ball milling 70 wt % CFO-PPy-NTs, 20 wt % Super-P (carbon black), 5 wt % polymerized styrene butadiene rubber (SBR), and 5 wt % carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) binder in deionized water. The weight ratio of the solid content is about 20 wt %. After being ball milled for 6 h, the slurry was coated onto the copper foil substrate using a doctor blade. Then the as-prepared electrode was dried under vacuum at 80 °C overnight and cut into round pieces of 12 mm in diameter by a cutting machine. The calculation of the mass loading is performed by cutting out the mass of the copper substrate, which is 12.25 mg in average, and only considering the weight ratio of the CFO-PPy-NTs (70 wt %). Thus, the average mass loading of CFO-PPy-NTs is 1.2 ± 0.2 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. A solution of 1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC) with 5 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) was employed as the electrolyte in this study. All the cells were assembled in a glovebox filled with purified argon gas with both a moisture and oxygen content below 0.1 ppm and measured at room temperature. Galvanostatic charge and discharge testing was conducted on a Land Test System (Wuhan, China) with a cutoff voltage ranging from 0.01 to 3.0 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) at various current densities. Cyclic voltammetry testing was carried out on an electrochemical workstation from 0.01 to 3.0 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3 Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">3.1 Phase and Morphology Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">The fabrication of CFO-PPy-NT is schematically illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>. The process starts with oriented self-assembly of MO molecules into a tubular supermolecule template. Then the pyrrole monomers are attached onto MO templates followed by further polymerization into PPy nanotubes. With the addition of Fe/Co precursors, CFO-PPy-NT can be obtained after a proper hydrothermal reaction along with postheat treatment.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/medium/am-2017-12503b_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/large/am-2017-12503b_0006.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Fabrication of the CFO-PPy-NT</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/large/am-2017-12503b_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b12503&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphology characterization of PPy-NTs and CFO-PPy-NTs were conducted using scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/suppl_file/am7b12503_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S1 and S2a</a>, an interconnected network of PPy nanotubes can be obtained via the MO assisted-template method. After the introduction of Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Co<sup>2+</sup> sources, CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles are uniformly distributed on the surface of PPy nanotubes (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a,b). For comparison, the SEM image of CFO-NPs is also presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/suppl_file/am7b12503_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>. As displayed by TEM image in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figures 1">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c,d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/suppl_file/am7b12503_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S2b</a>, the diameters of CFO-PPy-NTs increased a little after loading with CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles than with pure PPy-NTs (ca. 20 nm). The HRTEM image in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e further reveals that the roughened surface layer of CFO-PPy-NTs consists of CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles in the dimensions of 5 nm. Well-defined lattice fringes of (111), (220), (311), (222), and (400), are in accordance with <i>d</i>-spacings of 0.486, 0.297, 0.253, 0.241, and 0.208 nm, respectively. The uniform composition of CFO-PPy-NTs was also confirmed by energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) mapping in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f. As can be seen, the light points representing Co, Fe, and O have wider distribution areas than those of C and N, clearly indicating the homogeneous growth of CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles on the surface of PPy-NTs.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/medium/am-2017-12503b_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/large/am-2017-12503b_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a,b) SEM images of the CFO-PPy-NTs. TEM images of (c) PPy-NTs and (d) CFO-PPy-NTs. (e) HRTEM image of the CFO-NPs on CFO-PPy-NTs. (f) Electronic image and EDS elemental mapping analysis of the CFO-PPy-NTs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/large/am-2017-12503b_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b12503&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">All the characteristic peaks in the XRD pattern of CFO-PPy-NTs (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a) can be indexed to the spinel CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> structure (PDF No. 03–0864) without any impurity, similar to the pattern of bare CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> particles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/suppl_file/am7b12503_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). Particularly, the peak intensity of the (311) plane is obviously the highest, indicating preferred orientation, which is also explained in the literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> Absence of PPy peaks for CFO-PPy-NTs can be ascribed to the amorphous nature of PPy (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/suppl_file/am7b12503_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). The Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectrum of CFO-PPy-NTs is displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b. The band at 598 cm<sup>–1</sup> is attributed to intrinsic vibration of tetrahedral sites in metal oxides, which is corresponding with the Co/Fe–O bonding. The other bands can be assigned to N–H (3433, 1259 cm<sup>–1</sup>), C–H (2928, 1383, 806 cm<sup>–1</sup>), C═C (1622 cm<sup>–1</sup>), C–C and C–N (1454 cm<sup>–1</sup>), and a quinonoid structure (1103 cm<sup>–1</sup>), respectively, and are correlated with characteristic groups in PPy. Thus, the FTIR result further substantiates the existence of both PPy and CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> in CFO-PPy-NTs. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was conducted to study the chemical states of elements on the surface of PPy and CFO-PPy-NT. The C 1s and N 1s spectra of as-prepared PPy nanotubes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/suppl_file/am7b12503_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S6a and S6b</a>) are in good accordance with the reported spectra of PPy. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 28 to 30">(28-30)</a> For comparison, the corresponding spectra of CFO-PPy-NTs are also presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c,d. No obvious difference can be observed between bare PPy and CFO-PPy-NT in both C 1s and N 1s spectra. The binding energies of 285 and 286.8 eV correspond to C═C and C–N, respectively. Previous studies suggest that nitrogen atoms in pyrrole rings coordinated to the metal ions are responsible for enhancing the bonding between CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and the PPy matrix. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31, 32)</a> In addition, Co 2p and Fe 2p spectra of CFO-PPy-NTs are displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e,f. The fitting results of these two spectra suggest that the Fe in this composite exists as Fe<sup>3+</sup>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 33 to 35">(33-35)</a> while Co exists as Co<sup>2+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 36 to 39">(36-39)</a></div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/medium/am-2017-12503b_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/large/am-2017-12503b_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD pattern of CFO-PPy-NTs and the corresponding PDF. (b) FTIR analysis of CFO-PPy-NTs. High-resolution XPS spectra of (c) C 1s, (d) N 1s, (e) Co 2p, and (f) Fe 2p of CFO-PPy-NTs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/large/am-2017-12503b_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b12503&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) measurements were conducted in air as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a. The amount of PPy in the CFO-PPy-NTs was calculated to be 42.70 wt % according to the weight loss caused by the oxidation of PPy.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/medium/am-2017-12503b_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/large/am-2017-12503b_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) TGA curves of PPy-NTs and CFO-PPy-NTs. N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of (b) PPy-NTs and (c) CFO-PPy-NTs. (d) Pore size distribution of CFO-PPy-NTs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/large/am-2017-12503b_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b12503&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">As indicated by the nitrogen absorption–desorption isotherm curves of PPy and CFO-PPy-NT (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b,c), the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) specific surface area is enlarged after nanoparticle growth (from 14.04 to 43.39 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>). It can be seen that the diameters of most pores in PPy-NT are less than 2 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/suppl_file/am7b12503_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>), while the average diameter of the pores of CFO-PPy-NT is about 8 nm (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d). The Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) adsorption–desorption cumulative pore volumes of both PPy-NTs and CFO-PPy-NTs are also obtained through the analyzer and are 0.03 and 0.12 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/suppl_file/am7b12503_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8a,b</a>). Thus, the total pore volume of the CFO-PPy-NTs is much larger than that of the PPy-NTs. The larger surface area and pore volume will benefit the electrochemical performance owing to more active sites and contact between electrode and electrolyte.</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">3.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the potential application of the CFO-PPy-NTs as anode materials of SIBs, the galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were conducted with a cutoff voltage range from 0.01 to 3.0 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). When tested at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a), the initial discharge and charge capacities are 1096 and 614 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, resulting in the low initial Coulombic efficiency of 56%, which may be caused by irreversible decomposition of the electrolyte on the surface of the electrode <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> and the formation of irreversible Na<sub>2</sub>O due to the slow kinetics of sodium storage accompanied by the large radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> After 200 cycles, the CFO-PPy-NTs still retain a discharge capacity of 400 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, displaying high sodium storage capability and good cyclability. To identify the contributions of the PPy matrix to the sodium storage capacity of the CFO-PPy-NTs, bare PPy and CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> particles were also tested at the same current and voltage conditions. The contribution of PPy to the capacity of the CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>–PPy composite is negligible (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b). Meanwhile, the bare CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> can only deliver a capacity around 200 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, less than half of the capacity of CFO-PPy-NT, thus indicating that the introduction of PPy nanotubes has effectively maximized the conversion reaction of CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> without contributing extra capacity. Composites with different proportions of PPy were also tested to study the influence of the amount of PPy to the electrochemical performance. It is noted that the composites with initial PPy amounts of 0.1 and 0.2 g only deliver capacities of about 300 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, much lower that of the one with 0.15 g of initial PPy. To further study the cycle performance of the composite at a higher current density in the long term, the charge/discharge test was also conducted at 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). Even at such a high current density, the initial discharge capacity still remains at 502 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. After 2000 cycles, the capacity remains at 220 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, exhibiting good long-term cycle stability, owing to the interconnected conductive network of the PPy nanotubes.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/medium/am-2017-12503b_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/large/am-2017-12503b_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Cycle performance of CFO-PPy-NTs at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Cycle performance comparison among CFO-PPy-NTs with different amounts of PPy, bare CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> particles, and bare PPy-NTs. (c) Long-term cycle performance of CFO-PPy-NTs at 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Comparison between the rate performance of CFO-PPy-NTs and CFO-NPs. (e) EIS study of both CFO-PPy-NTs and CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> particles before and after cycling.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/large/am-2017-12503b_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b12503&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Furthermore, the rate performance of the CFO-PPy-NTs was evaluated by increasing the current density from 0.05 up to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> and finally turning it back to 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>, reversely. As can be seen in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d, the discharge capacity of the composite decreases from 767 (0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>), to 542 (0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>), 471 (0.3 A g<sup>–1</sup>), 421 (0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>), 370 (1 A g<sup>–1</sup>), 272 (3 A g<sup>–1</sup>), 240 (5 A g<sup>–1</sup>) and 189 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (10 A g<sup>–1</sup>), respectively. The capacity successfully returned to 656 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> when the current density is back to 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating excellent robustness of this composite. For comparison, the rate performance of CFO-NPs was also tested. It is clear that the rate performance of the CFO-PPy-NTs is far better than that of the CFO-NPs due to enhanced conductivity by introducing PPy nanotubes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 42 to 44">(42-44)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">As <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/suppl_file/am7b12503_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a> is displayed, the electrochemical reactions of the CFO-PPy-NTs were investigated by cyclic voltammetry (CV) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in a voltage range from 0.01 to 3 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. During the initial scan, the cathodic peak at 0.38 V is related to the reduction of Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Co<sup>2+</sup> into metallic Fe and Co, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref45" aria-label="reference 11 and 45">(11, 45)</a> while the broad anodic peak centered at 1.51 V can be attributed to the oxidation of metallic Fe and Co. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> The curves of the following scans obtain good conformity. In conclusion, the significant difference between the initial and the following scans implies the initial irreversible electrochemical reactions and subsequent favorable reversibility of the composite, indicating good accordance with the cycling performance in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was conducted to further verify the contributions of PPy nanotubes to the conductivity of the CFO-PPy-NT electrode. As is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e (equivalent circuit in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/suppl_file/am7b12503_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>), the semicircle at high frequency is correlated with the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), which represents the impedance corresponding with sodium ion migration through the surface film and charge transfer through the electrode/electrolyte interfaces. After 200 cycles, the radius of the depressed semicircle for bare CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> has been obviously enlarged compared with the fresh one, corresponding with the increasing <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> from 230 to 511 Ω. As for CFO-PPy-NTs, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> increased from 118 to 220 Ω, demonstrating a much lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> before cycling and a smaller increase after 200 cycles, indicating good accordance with the electrochemical performance discussed above, exhibiting a beneficial effect from the highly conductive network of interconnected PPy nanotubes.</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12">3.3 Compositional and Morphological Characterization of Cycled Products</h3><div class="NLM_p">The composition and morphology of cycled products were characterized to further study the reaction mechanism, elucidating the relationship between the good electrochemical performance and the hierarchical nanostructure. The existence of Co<sup>2+</sup> was confirmed by XPS for the cycled CFO-PPy-NT electrode (charged state) in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/suppl_file/am7b12503_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11a</a>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref47 ref48 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47 ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 46 to 49">(46-49)</a> However, an additional peak attributed to Fe<sup>2+</sup> at 714.7 eV appears <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> in the Fe 2p spectrum (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/suppl_file/am7b12503_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11b</a>) apart from three other peaks ascribed to Fe<sup>3+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51 ref52 ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51 ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 51 to 53">(51-53)</a> According to previous reports, the XPS results indicate the existence of CoO, while Fe might suggest the existence of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and FeO. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54 ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54 ref55" aria-label="reference 54 and 55">(54, 55)</a> And the reaction mechanism is proposed as follows (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eqs 1">eqs </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>–<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="4">4</a>)<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/am-2017-12503b_m001.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/am-2017-12503b_m002.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/am-2017-12503b_m003.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq4"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/am-2017-12503b_m004.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></span></div><div class="NLM_p">The cycled electrode of CFO-PPy-NT retains an interconnected network structure after cycling, with a uniform distribution of nanoparticles on the nanotubes (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a and the inset, pristine electrode in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/suppl_file/am7b12503_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S12a and S12b</a>), displaying good structure stability, leading to stable long-term cyclability. Compared with CFO-PPy-NTs, there are obvious cracks on the cycled electrode of bare CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> particles, where many particles detach from each other (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b and the inset, pristine electrode in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/suppl_file/am7b12503_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S13a and S13b</a>), resulting in inferior conductivity and poor cycle stability.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/medium/am-2017-12503b_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/large/am-2017-12503b_0005.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. FESEM images of cycled electrodes of (a) CFO-PPy-NTs and (b) CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> particles. The insets are corresponding images at higher magnification. Both electrodes were cycled at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles. (c) TEM, (d) SAED, (e) HRTEM images, and (f) electronic image and EDS elemental distribution of the cycled CFO-PPy-NTs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b12503/asset/images/large/am-2017-12503b_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b12503&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The nanotube structure after cycling is further confirmed by TEM images in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c. In the selected area electronic diffraction (SAED) image (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d), the (220), (200), and (002) planes of FeO and the (220), (200) planes of CoO can be clearly observed in the polycrystalline diffraction pattern, while the (440) and (220) planes of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> are also detected. This result is in good accordance with the XPS analysis, providing more evidence for the composition of the cycled products. As can be seen from the HRTEM image (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e), the corresponding lattice planes in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d are also observed. There are no obvious oversized particles on PPy nanotubes, retaining their original sizes (around 5 nm). Thus, the initial well-distributed CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles on the PPy nanotubes have effectively avoided the severe agglomeration and oversized particle growth that may cause pulverization and decline in the electrical conductivity of the electrode. The EDS elemental mapping displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f indicates good distribution of product particles on the surface of PPy nanotubes after cycling.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Based on the above postcycle analysis, we can conclude that the PPy nanotube structure favors fast electronic transportation, enhancing the conductivity and thus improving the rate performance. The high specific area provides more active sites for Na<sup>+</sup>. Uniquely, the nanoparticles anchored on the PPy-NTs avoid severe agglomeration and electrode degradation, maintaining good cyclability.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">4 Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles were successfully coated on the surfaces of the interconnected PPy nanotube network through a facile and environmentally friendly hydrothermal method. The as-prepared CFO-PPy-NT network displays excellent electrochemical performance as the anode material for SIBs, especially with regards to the high capacity and stable cyclability (400 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and 220 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 2000 cycles at 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), high Coulombic efficiency (98%), and good rate capability (189 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>). These results are due to the homogeneous growth of CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles on the PPy matrix with an interconnected 3-D structure, which can greatly enhance the electronic conductivity of the composite and act as a buffer to relieve the strain caused by the volume changes in the electrode during the cycling. At the meantime, the diffusion of sodium ions is also favored. For comparison, the bare CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles prepared with the same method delivered much lower capacity. Thus, these results obviously indicate that the coated CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> particles and the PPy nanotube network generate a great synergetic effect during the cycling, enabling maximum utilization of the sodium storage capability of CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles. Based on the excellent performance discussed above, it is really hopeful for such kinds of transitional metal oxide–conductive polymer nanocomposites to be promising candidates of anode materials for future sodium ion batteries.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.7b12503" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.7b12503</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images of PPy-NTs; TEM images of PPy-NTs and CFO-PPy-NTs at low magnification; SEM image of CoFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles; comparison between the XRD patterns of CFO-NPs and CFO-PPy-NTs; XRD pattern of PPy-NTs; high-resolution XPS spectra of PPy-NTs including C 1s and N 1s; pore size distribution of PPy-NTs; BJH adsorption–desorption cumulative pore volumes of PPy-NTs and CFO-PPy-NTs; CV scan curves of the CFO-PPy-NTs;
